      A B LOCK C OORDINATE D ESCENT M ETHOD FOR

008
009                                                A BSTRACT
010
011               Nonsmooth composite optimization with orthogonality constraints is crucial in
012               statistical learning and data science, but it presents challenges due to its nons-
013
                  mooth objective and computationally expensive, non-convex constraints. In this
                  paper, we propose a new approach called OBCD, which leverages Block Coor-
014
                  dinate Descent (BCD) to address these challenges. OBCD is a feasible method
015
                  with a small computational footprint. In each iteration, it updates k rows of the
016               solution matrix, where k ≥ 2, while globally solving a small nonsmooth op-
017               timization problem under orthogonality constraints. We prove that the limiting
018               points of OBCD, referred to as (global) block-k stationary points, offer stronger
019               optimality than standard critical points. Furthermore, we show that OBCD con-
020               verges to ϵ-block-k stationary points with an ergodic convergence rate of O(1/ϵ).
021               Additionally, under the Kurdyka-Lojasiewicz (KL) inequality, we establish the
022               non-ergodic convergence rate of OBCD. We also extend OBCD with breakpoint
023               searching methods for subproblem solving and greedy strategies for working set
024
                  selection. Comprehensive experiments demonstrate the superior performance of
                  our approach across various tasks.
025
026
027
      1       I NTRODUCTION
028
029
      We consider the following nonsmooth composite optimization problem under orthogonality con-
030
      straints (‘≜’ means define):
031
032                              min    F (X) ≜ f (X) + h(X), s.t. XT X = Ir .                               (1)
                               X∈Rn×r
033
034   Here, n ≥ r and Ir is a r × r identity matrix. We do not assume convexity of f (X) and h(X).
035   For brevity, the orthogonality constraints XT X = Ir in Problem (1) is rewritten as X ∈ St(n, r) ≜
036   {X ∈ Rn×r | XT X = Ir }, where M ≜ St(n, r) is the Stiefel manifold in the literature (Edelman
037   et al., 1998; Absil et al., 2008; Wen & Yin, 2013; Hu et al., 2020). We impose the following
038   assumptions on Problem (1) throughout this paper. (Asm-i) For any X and X+ , where X and
039   X+ only differ at most by k rows with k ≥ 2, we assume f : Rn×r 7→ R is H-smooth with
040   0 ⪯ H ∈ Rnr×nr such that:
041
                       f (X+ ) ≤ Q(X+ ; X) ≜ f (X) + ⟨X+ − X, ∇f (X)⟩ + 12 ∥X+ − X∥2H ,                      (2)
042
043   where ∥H∥sp ≤ Lf for some constant Lf > 0 and ∥X∥2H ≜ vec(X)T Hvec(X) 1 . Here, ∥H∥sp is
044   the spectral norm of H. Notably, when H = Lf · Inr , this condition simplifies to the standard Lf -
045   smoothness (Nesterov, 2003). (Asm-ii) The function h(X) : Rn×r 7→ R is closed, proper, and lower
046   semicontinuous,
               P       and potentially non-smooth. Additionally, it is coordinate-wise separable, such that
047   h(X) = i,j h(Xij ). Typical examples of h(X) include the ℓp norm function h(X) = ∥X∥p with
048   p ∈ {0, 1}, and the indicator function for non-negativity constraints h(X) = I≥0 (X). (Asm-iii)
049   The following small-sized subproblem can be solved exactly and efficiently:
050
                                  min      P(V) ≜ 21 ∥V∥2Q + ⟨V, P⟩ + h(VZ),                                 (3)
051                            V∈St(k,k)
052
053
          1
              Given any symmetric matrices C ∈ Rn×n and D ∈ Rr×r , we let H = D ⊗ C. The function f (X) =
      1
      2
        tr(XT CXD) = 12 ∥X∥2H satisfies (2) with equality, as f (X+ ) = Q(X+ ; X) holds for all X and X+ .

      for any given Z ∈ Rk×r
                           P, P ∈ R
                                    k×k
                                        , and Q ∈ Rk ×k . Here, we employ a notational simplification
055
      by defining h(VZ) ≜ i,j h([VZ]ij ), given the coordinate-wise separability of the function h(·).
056
057   Problem (1) is an optimization framework that plays a crucial role in a variety of statistical learn-
058   ing and data science models, such as sparse Principal Component Analysis (PCA) (Journée et al.,
059   2010; Shalit & Chechik, 2014), nonnegative PCA (Zass & Shashua, 2006; Qian et al., 2021), deep
060   neural networks (Cogswell et al., 2016; Cho & Lee, 2017; Xie et al., 2017; Bansal et al., 2018;
061
      Massart & Abrol, 2022; Huang & Gao, 2023), electronic structure calculation (Zhang et al., 2014;
      Liu et al., 2014), Fourier transforms approximation (Frerix & Bruna, 2019), phase synchronization
062
      (Liu et al., 2017), orthogonal nonnegative matrix factorization (Jiang et al., 2022), K-indicators
063
      clustering (Jiang et al., 2016), and dictionary learning (Zhai et al., 2020).
064
065
      1.1   R ELATED W ORK
066   We now present some related algorithms in the literature.
067
      ▶ Minimizing Smooth Functions under Orthogonality Constraints. One difficulty in solving
068   Problem (1) arises from the nonconvexity of the orthogonality constraints. Existing methods for han-
069   dling this issue can be divided into three classes. (i) Geodesic-like methods (Abrudan et al., 2008;
070   Edelman et al., 1998; Absil et al., 2008; Jiang & Dai, 2015). Since calculating geodesics involves
071   solving ordinary differential equations, which may cause computational complexity, geodesic-like
072   methods iteratively compute the geodesic logarithm using simple linear algebra calculations. The
073   work of (Wen & Yin, 2013) develops a simple and efficient constraint preserving update scheme
074   and achieves low computation complexity per iteration. They combine the feasible update scheme
075   with the Barzilai-Borwein (BB) nonmonotonic line search for optimization with orthogonality con-
076
      straints. (ii) Projection-like methods (Absil et al., 2008; Golub & Van Loan, 2013). These methods
      preserve the orthogonality constraints by projection. They decrease the objective value using its
077
      current Euclidean gradient direction or Riemannian tangent direction, followed by an orthogonal
078
      projection operation. This can be calculated by polar decomposition or approximated by QR fac-
079   torization. (iii) Multiplier correction methods (Gao et al., 2018; 2019; Xiao et al., 2022). Since the
080   Lagrangian multiplier associated with the orthogonality constraint is symmetric and has an explicit
081   closed-form expression at the first-order optimality condition, multiplier correction methods update
082   the multiplier after achieving sufficient reduction in the objective function. This leads to efficient
083   first-order feasible or infeasible approaches.
084
      ▶ Minimizing Nonmooth Functions under Orthogonality Constraints. Another difficulty of
085   solving Problem (1) comes from the nonsmoothness of the objective function. Existing methods
086   for addressing this problem can be classified into three categories. (i) Subgradient methods (Hwang
087   et al., 2015; Li et al., 2021; Cheung et al., 2024). Subgradient methods are analogous to gradient
088   descent methods. Most of the aforementioned geodesic-like and projection-like strategies can be
089   incorporated into the subgradient methods. However, the step size in subgradient methods needs to
090   be diminishing to guarantee convergence. (ii) Proximal gradient methods (Chen et al., 2020; Li et al.,
091   2024). They solve a strongly convex minimization problem over the tangent space using a semi-
092   smooth Newton method to find a descent direction. Subsequently, they maintain the orthogonality
093
      constraint through a retraction operation. (iii) Block Majorization Minimization (BMM) or BCD
      on Riemannian manifolds (Li et al., 2024; 2023; Breloy et al., 2021; Gutman & Ho-Nguyen, 2023;
094
      Cheung et al., 2024). This class of methods iteratively constructs a tangential majorizing surrogate
095
      for a block of the objective function, takes an approximate descent step in the resulting direction
096   within the tangent space, and then applies retraction to project back onto the manifold. Notably,
097   their subproblems are often solved approximately, whereas our method can solve them exactly due
098   to the small size of the subproblems. (iv) Operator splitting methods (Lai & Osher, 2014; Chen et al.,
099   2016; Zhang et al., 2019). Operator splitting methods introduce linear constraints and decompose the
100   original problem into simpler subproblems, which can be solved separately and exactly. Alternating
101   Direction Methods of Multipliers (ADMM) (He & Yuan, 2012) and Smoothing Penalty Methods
102   (SPM) (Chen, 2012) represent two prominent variants of operator splitting methods.
103   ▶ Block Coordinate Descent Methods. (Block) coordinate descent is a classical and powerful
104   algorithm that solves optimization problems by iteratively performing minimization along (block)
105   coordinate directions (Tseng & Yun, 2009; Xu & Yin, 2013). The BCD methods have recently
106   gained attention in solving nonconvex optimization problems, including sparse optimization (Yuan,
107   2024), k-means clustering (Nie et al., 2022), structured nonconvex minimization (Yuan, 2023), re-
      current neural network (Massart & Abrol, 2022), and multi-layer convolutional networks (Bibi et al.,


                                                        2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      2019; Zeng et al., 2019). BCD methods have also been used in (Shalit & Chechik, 2014; Massart &
109   Abrol, 2022) for solving optimization problems with orthogonal group constraints. However, their
110   column-wise BCD methods are limited only to solve smooth minimization problems with k = 2
111   and r = n (Refer to Section 4.2 in (Shalit & Chechik, 2014)). Our row-wise BCD methods can
112   solve general nonsmooth problems with k ≥ 2 and r ≤ n. The work of (Gao et al., 2019) proposes
113   a parallelizable column-wise BCD scheme for solving the subproblems of their proximal linearized
114   augmented Lagrangian algorithm. Impressive parallel scalability in a parallel environment of their
115   algorithm is demonstrated. We stress that our row-wise BCD methods differ from the two column-
116   wise counterparts.
117   ▶ Summary. Existing solutions have one or more of the following limitations: (i) They rely on
118   full gradient information, incurring high computational costs per iteration. (ii) They cannot han-
119   dle general nonsmooth composite problems. (iii) They lack descent properties, even worse, they
120   are infeasible methods, achieving solution feasibility only at the limit point. (iv) They often lack
121   rigorous convergence guarantees. (v) They only establish weak optimality at critical points. ⋆
122   To our knowledge, this represents the first application of BCD methods to solve nonsmooth com-
123
      posite optimization problems under orthogonality constraints, demonstrating strong optimality and
      convergence guarantees.
124
125
126
      1.2    C ONTRIBUTIONS
127   This paper makes the following contributions. (i) Algorithmically: We propose a Block Coordi-
128   nate Descent (BCD) algorithm tailored for nonsmooth composite optimization under orthogonality
129   constraints (Section 2). (ii) Theoretically: We provide comprehensive optimality and convergence
130   analyses of our methods (Sections 3 and 4). (iii) Side Contributions: We introduce breakpoint
131   searching methods for solving subproblems when k = 2 (Section 5), and present two working set
132   selection greedy strategies to improve the computational efficiency of our methods (Section D in the
133   Appendix). (iv) Empirically: Extensive experiments demonstrate that our methods surpass existing
134   solutions in terms of accuracy and/or efficiency (Section 6).
135
136   2     T HE P ROPOSED OBCD A LGORITHM
137
138   In this section, we introduce OBCD, a Block Coordinate Descent algorithm for solving general
139   nonsmooth composite problems under Orthogonality constraints, as defined in Problem 1.
140
      We start by presenting a new update scheme designed to maintain the orthogonality constraint.

      ▶ The Main Algorithm. The proposed algorithm OBCD is an iterative procedure that sequentially
178   minimizes the objective function along block coordinate directions within a sub-manifold of M.
179
180   Starting with an initial feasible solution, OBCD iteratively determines a working set Bt using spe-
181
      cific strategies. It then solves the small-sized subproblem in Problem (7) through successive ma-
      jorization minimization. This method iteratively constructs a surrogate function that majorizes the
182
      objective function, driving it to decrease as expected (Mairal, 2013; Razaviyayn et al., 2013; Sun
183
      et al., 2016; Breloy et al., 2021), and it has proven effective for minimizing complex functions.
184
185   We now demonstrate how to derive the majorization function for F (XBt (V)) in Problem (7). Initially,

186   for any Xt ∈ St(n, r) and V ∈ St(k, k), we establish following inequalities: f (XBt (V)) − f (Xt ) ≤

190   Claim (a) of Lemma 2.2; step ③ uses α > 0 and Q ⪯ Q, which can be ensured by choosing Q

206
      Three strategies to find the working set B with |B| = k can be considered. (i) Random strategy: B
      is randomly selected from {B1 , B2 , ..., BCkn } with equal probability 1/Ckn . (ii) Cyclic strategy: Bt
207
      takes all possible combinations in cyclic order, such as B1 → B2 → ... → BCkn → B1 → .... (iii)
208
      Greedy strategy: We propose two novel greedy strategies to find a good working set. Due to space
209   limitation, we have included them in Appendix D.
210

212
      dient method with low iterative computational complexity as it only assesses k rows of the Euclidean
      gradient of ∇f (Xt ) and the solution Xt to compute the linear term ⟨[∇f (Xt )(Xt )T ]BB , V⟩ =

237   Remark 2.4. (a) By Claim (b) of Lemma 2.3, when k > 2, h(X) = 0, and Q is chosen to be a
238   diagonal matrix as in Equation (9), the subproblem V̄t ∈ arg minV∈St(k,k) K(V; Xt , B) in Algo-
239   rithm 1 can be solved exactly and efficiently due to our assumption, see Remark 2.6. (b) For general
240   k and h(·), the subproblem may not be solved globally, but a local stationary solution V̄t satisfying
241   (V̄t ; Xt , B) ≤ K(Ik ; Xt , B) can be achieved. Although strong optimality may be compromised,
242   convergence to a critical point (as discussed later) for the final solution X∞ remains achievable.

      ▶ Smallest Possible Subproblems When k = 2. We now discuss how to solve the subproblems
244
      exactly when k = 2 and h(·) ̸= 0. The following lemma reveals an equivalent expression for any
245
      V ∈ St(2, 2).
246
247
      Lemma 2.5. (Proof in Appendix E.4) Any orthogonal matrix V ∈ St(2, 2) can be expressed as
                                                              cos(θ) sin(θ)              − cos(θ) sin(θ)
248   V = Vθrot or V = Vθref for some θ ∈ R, where Vθrot ≜ ( − sin(θ) cos(θ) ), Vθref ≜ ( sin(θ) cos(θ) ).
249   We have det(Vθrot ) = 1 and det(Vθref ) = −1 for any θ.
250
251   Using Lemma 2.5, we can reformulate Problem (3) as the following one-dimensional problem:
252   θ̄ ∈ arg minθ P(V), s.t. V ∈ {Vθrot , Vθref }. The optimal solution θ̄ can be identified even if
253
      h(·) ̸= 0 using a novel breakpoint searching method, which is discussed later in Section 5.
254   Remark 2.6. (i) Vθrot and Vθref are called Givens rotation matrix and Jacobi reflection matrix
255   respectively in the literature (Sun & Bischof, 1995). Previous research only considered {Vθrot } for
256   solving symmetric linear eigenvalue problems (Golub & Van Loan, 2013) and sparse PCA problems
257
      (Shalit & Chechik, 2014), while we use {Vθref , Vθrot } for solving Problem (1). (ii) We show the
258
      necessity of using {Vθref , Vθrot } in the following two examples of 2 × 2 optimization problems with
259   orthogonality constraints: minV∈St(2,2) F (V) ≜ ∥V − A∥2F , and minV∈St(2,2) F (V) ≜ ∥V −
                                          1 0
260   B∥2F + 5∥V∥1 , where A = ( −1                           10                                      ref
                                            −1 ) and B = ( 1 2 ). The use of the reflection matrix Vθ is
261   essential in these examples because it results in lower objective values. See Section C.1 in the
262
      Appendix for more details.

264   3    O PTIMALITY A NALYSIS
265   This section provides some optimality analysis for the proposed algorithm.
266
      ▶ Basis Representation of Orthogonal Matrices. The following theorem is used to characterize
267
      any orthogonal matrix D ∈ St(n, n) and X ∈ St(n, r).
268
269   Theorem 3.1. (Proof in Appendix F.1, Basis Representation of Orthogonal Matrices) Assume k = 2.
      For all i ∈ [Ckn ], we define Wi ≜ In + UBi (Vi − Ik )UT                 T            T
                                                                 Bi = UBi Vi UBi + UBi UBc , where
                                                                                         c
                                                                                                        i

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      Vi ∈ St(2, 2). We have: (a) Any matrix D ∈ St(n, n) can be expressed as D = WCkn ...W2 W1
271   using suitable Wi (which depends on Vi ). Furthermore, if ∀i, Vi = I2 , then D = In . (b) Any
272   matrix X ∈ St(n, r) can be expressed as X = WCkn ...W2 W1 X0 using suitable Wi and any fixed
273   constant matrix X0 ∈ St(n, r).
274
275
      Remark 3.2. (i) We use both Givens rotation and Jacobi reflection matrices to compute D ∈
      St(n, n). This is necessary since a reflection matrix cannot be represented through a sequence
276
      of rotations. (ii) The result in Claim (b) of Theorem 3.1 indicates that the proposed update
277
      scheme X+ ⇐ X + UB (V − Ik )UT        B X as shown in Formula (5) can reach any orthogonal ma-
278   trix X ∈ St(n, r) for any starting solution X0 ∈ St(n, r).
279
280   ▶ First-Order Optimality Conditions for Problem (1). We provide the first-order optimality con-
281   dition of Problem (1) (Wen & Yin, 2013; Chen et al., 2020). We use ∂F (X) to denote the limiting
282   subdifferential of F (X) (Mordukhovich, 2006; Rockafellar & Wets., 2009), which is always non-
283   empty since F (X) is closed, proper, and lower semicontinuous. Given f (X) is differentiable, we
284   have ∂F (X) = ∂(f + h)(X) = ∇f (X) + ∂h(X). We extend the definition of limiting subdiffer-
285
      ential to introduce ∂M F (X) as the Riemannian limiting subdifferential of F (X) at X, defined as
286   ∂M F (X) ≜ ∂F (X) ⊖ (X[∂F (X)]T X), where ⊖ is the element-wise subtraction between sets.
287   Introducing a Lagrangian multiplier matrix Λ ∈ Rr×r for the orthogonality constraint, we define
288   the following Lagrangian function of Problem (1): L(X, Λ) = F (X) + 12 ⟨Ir − XT X, Λ⟩. Notable,
289   the matrix Λ is symmetric, as XT X is symmetric. We state the following definition of first-order
290   optimality condition.
291
      Definition 3.3. Critical Point (Wen & Yin, 2013; Chen et al., 2020). A solution X̌ ∈ St(n, r) is
292
      a critical point of Problem (1) if: 0 ∈ ∂M F (X̌) ≜ ∂F (X̌) ⊖ (X̌[∂F (X̌)]T X̌), where (∂F (X̌) ⊖
293
      X̌[∂F (X̌)]T X̌) ≜ {G − X̌GT X̌ | G ∈ ∂F (X̌)}. Furthermore, Λ ∈ [∂F (X̌)]T X̌.
294
295   Remark 3.4. The critical point condition in Lemma 3.3 can be equivalently expressed as (Absil
296   et al., 2008; Jiang & Dai, 2015; Liu et al., 2016): 0 ∈ PTX̌ M (∂F (X̌)). Here, TX M is the tangent
297   space to M at X ∈ M with TX M = {Y ∈ Rn×r | XT Y + YT X = 0}.
298
      ▶ Optimality Conditions for the Subproblems. The Euclidean subdifferential of K(V; Xt , Bt )
299
      w.r.t. V can be computed as follows: G̈(V) ≜ ∆      ¨ + UT [∇f (Xt ) + ∂h(Xt+1 )](Xt )T UB , where
300                                                               B
       ¨
      ∆ = mat((Q+αIk )vec(V −Ik )), and X       t+1
                                                     = Xt +UB (V −Ik )UT          t
301                                                                           B X . Using Lemma 3.3, we set
                                                t    t
      the Riemannian subdifferential of K(V; X , B ) w.r.t. V to zero and obtain the following first-order
302
303
      optimality condition for V̄t : 0 ∈ ∂M K(V̄t ; Xt , Bt ) ≜ G̈(V̄t ) ⊖ V̄t G̈(V̄t )T V̄t .
304   ▶ Optimality Conditions and Their Hierarchy. We introduce the following new optimality con-
305   dition of block-k stationary points.
306   Definition 3.5. (Global) Block-k Stationary Point, abbreviated as BSk -point. Let α > 0 and
307                                                                                             Ck
      k ≥ 2. A solution Ẍ ∈ St(n, r) is called a block-k stationary point if: ∀B ∈ {Bi }i=1
                                                                                          n
                                                                                             , Ik ∈
308
309
      arg minV∈St(k,k) K(V; Ẍ, B), where K(·; ·, ·) is defined in Equation (10).
310
311
      Remarks. BSk -point states that if we globally minimize the majorization function K(V; Ẍ, B),
312   there is no possibility of improving the objective function value for K(V; Ẍ, B) across all B ∈
           Ck
313   {Bi }i=1
            n
               .
314
      The following theorem establishes the relation between BSk -points, standard critical points, and
315   global optimal points.
316
      Theorem 3.6. (Proof in Appendix F.2) We establish the following relationships:
317
318   (a) {critical points X̌} ⊇ {BS2 -points Ẍ}.
319   (b) {BS2 -points Ẍ} ⊇ {global optimal points X̄}.
320
      (c) {BSk -points Ẍ} ⊇ {BSk+1 -points Ẍ}, where k ∈ {2, 3, . . . , n − 1}.
321
      (d) The reverse of the above three inclusions may not always hold true.
322
323   Remark 3.7. The optimality of BS2 -points is stronger than that of standard critical points (Wen &
      Yin, 2013; Chen et al., 2020; Absil et al., 2008).

      4       C ONVERGENCE A NALYSIS
325
326   This section presents the ergodic and non-ergodic (or last-iterate) convergence rates of the proposed
327   OBCD algorithm.
328
      We denote any point of the limit point set of OBCD (which is not necessarily a singleton) as Ẍ.
329   For the case where a random strategy is used to find the working set, OBCD generates a random
330   output (V̄t , Xt+1 ) with t = 0, 1, . . . , ∞ which depends on the observed realization of the random
331   variable: ξ t ≜ (B1 , B2 , B3 , . . . , Bt ).
332
333
      4.1     E RGODIC C ONVERGENCE R ATE
334
335   Initially, we introduce the notation of ϵ-BSk -point as follows.
336
      Definition 4.1. (ϵ-BSk -point) Given any constant ϵ > 0, a point Ẍ is called an ϵ-BSk -point if:
337      PCkn
       1                                        2
338   Ck  n
            i=1 dist(Ik , arg minV K(V; X, Bi )) ≤ ϵ, where K(·; ·, ·) is defined in Equation (10).
339
340
      Using the optimality measure from Definition 4.1, we establish the ergodic convergence rates of
      OBCD.
341
342   Theorem 4.2. (Proof in Appendix G.1) We define c̃ ≜ α2 · (F (X0 ) − F (Ẍ)). We have:
343   (a) The following sufficient decrease condition holds for all t ≥ 0:
344
                              α

348                                         k

                                                                             k
350        most T iterations deterministically, where T ≥ ⌈ c̃ϵ + Ckn ⌉.
351   Remark 4.3. Theorem 4.2 shows that OBCD converges to ϵ-block-k stationary points with an er-
352   godic convergence rate of O(1/ϵ), which is typical for general nonconvex optimization.
353

      is employed to determine the working set in the remainder of this paper.

      4.2     N ON - ERGODIC CONVERGENCE RATE UNDER KL A SSUMPTION
373
374   We establish the non-ergodic convergence rate of OBCD using the Kurdyka-Łojasiewicz inequality,
375   a key tool in non-convex analysis (Attouch et al., 2010; Bolte et al., 2014; Liu et al., 2016).
376
      Initially, we make the following additional assumption.
377
      Assumption 4.7. The function F ◦ (X) = F (X) + IM (X) is a KL function.


                                                         7
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


378
      Remark 4.8. Semi-algebraic functions are a class of functions that satisfy the KL property. These
379   functions are widely used in applications, and they include real polynomial functions, finite sums
380   and products of semi-algebraic functions, and indicator functions of semi-algebraic sets (Attouch
381   et al., 2010; Xu & Yin, 2013).
382
383   We present the following useful proposition, due to (Attouch et al., 2010; Bolte et al., 2014).
384   Proposition 4.9. (Kurdyka-Łojasiewicz Property). For a KL function F ◦ (X) with X ∈ dom F ◦ ,
385   there exists σ ∈ [0, 1), η ∈ (0, +∞], a neighborhood Υ of Ẍ, and a concave continuous function
386   φ(t) = ct1−σ , c > 0, t ∈ [0, η) such that for all X′ ∈ Υ and satisfies F ◦ (X′ ) ∈ (F ◦ (Ẍ), F ◦ (Ẍ) +
387   η), the following inequality holds: dist(0, ∂F ◦ (X′ ))φ′ (F ◦ (X′ ) − F ◦ (Ẍ)) ≥ 1.
388
389   Utilizing the Kurdyka-Łojasiewicz property, one can establish a finite-length property of OBCD, a
390   result considerably stronger than that of Theorem 4.2.
391
      Theorem 4.10. P (Proof in Appendix G.4, A Finite Length Property). We define et+1 ≜ Eξt [∥V̄t −
392                     ∞
      Ik ∥F ], and d = j=i ej+1 . Based on the continuity assumption made in Lemma 4.4, We have:
                    i
393
394   (a) It holds that (et+1 )2 ≤ κet (φt − φt+1 ), where φt ≜ φ(F (Xt ) − F (Ẍ)), κ ≜ 2γϕα is a positive
395       constant, γ ≜ (Ckn /Ck−2    ) 1/2
                                            , ϕ is defined in Lemma 4.4, and φ(·) is the  desingularization
                                  n−2
396       function defined in Proposition 4.9.
397   (b) It holds that ∀t ≥ 1, dt ≤ et + 2κφt . The sequence {et }∞t=1 has the finite length property that
398             P∞
          dt ≜ j=t ej+1 is always upper-bounded by a certain constant.
399
400   Finally, we establish the last-iterate convergence rate for OBCD.
401
      Theorem 4.11. (Proof in Appendix G.5). Based on the continuity assumption made in Lemma 4.4,
402   there exists t′ such that for all t ≥ t′ , we have:
403
404   (a) If σ = 0, then the sequence Xt converges in a finite number of steps in expectation.
405   (b) If σ ∈ (0, 12 ], then there exist ċ > 0 and τ̇ ∈ [0, 1) such that Eξt−1 [∥Xt − X∞ ∥F ] ≤ ċτ̇ t .
406   (c) If σ ∈ ( 21 , 1), then there exist ċ > 0 such that Eξt−1 [∥Xt − X∞ ∥F ] ≤ O(t−(1−σ)/(2σ−1) ).
407   Remark 4.12. When F (X) is a semi-algebraic function and the desingularising function is φ(t) =
408   ct1−σ for some c > 0 and σ ∈ [0, 1), Theorem 4.11 shows that OBCD converges in finite iterations
409   when σ = 0, with linear convergence when σ ∈ (0, 12 ], and sublinear convergence when σ ∈ ( 12 , 1)
410   for the gap ∥Xt − X∞ ∥F in expectation. These results are consistent with those in (Attouch et al.,
411   2010).

413   5       S OLVING THE S UBPROBLEM WHEN k = 2
414
      This section presents a novel Breakpoint Searching Method (BSM) to find the global optimal solu-
415
      tion of Problem (3) when k = 2.
416
417   Initially, Problem (3) boils down to the following one-dimensional subproblem: minθ 12 ∥V∥2Q +
418   ⟨V, P⟩ + h(VZ), s.t. V ∈ {Vθrot , Vθref }, which can be further rewritten as: θ̄ ∈
                                                                     ± cos(θ) sin(θ)
419   arg minθ 21 vec(V)T Qvec(V) + ⟨V, P⟩ + h(VZ), s.t. V ≜ ( ∓ sin(θ) cos(θ) ), where Q ∈ R4×4 ,
420   P ∈ R2×2 , and Z ∈ R2×r . Given h(·) is coordinate-wise separable, we have the following equiva-
421   lent optimization problem:
422
423   min h (cos(θ)x + sin(θ)y) + a cos(θ) + b sin(θ) + c cos2 (θ) + d cos(θ) sin(θ) + e sin2 (θ),             (11)
          θ
424
425   where a = P22 ±P11 , b = P12 ∓P21 , c = 0.5(Q11 +Q44 )±Q14 , d = −Q12 ±Q13 ∓Q24 +Q34 ,
426   e = 0.5(Q22 + Q33 ) ∓ Q23 , r = ±Z(1, :), s = Z(2, :), p = Z(2, :), u = ∓Z(1, :), x ≜ [r; p] ∈
427   R2r×1 , and y ≜ [s; u] ∈ R2r×1 .
428   Our key strategy is to perform a variable substitution to convert Problem (11) into an equivalent
429   problem that depends on thepvariable tan(θ) ≜ t. The substitutionpis based on the trigonometric
430
      identities that cos(θ) = ±1/ 1 + tan2 (θ) and sin(θ) = ± tan(θ)/ 1 + tan2 (θ).
431
      The following lemma provides a characterization of the global optimal solution for Problem (11).

442   We describe our BSM to solve Problem (12); our approach can be naturally extended to tackle
443   Problem (13). BSM first identifies all the possible breakpoints / critical points Θ, and then picks the
444   solution that leads to the lowest value as the optimal solution t̄, i.e., t̄ ∈ arg mint p(t), s.t. t ∈ Θ.
445
      We assume yi ̸= 0. If this is not true and there exists yi = 0 for some i, then {xi , yi } can be
446   removed since it does not affect the minimizer of the problem.
447
448   We now show that how to find the breakpoint set Θ for h(x) = λ∥x∥0 , where λ ≥ 0. We also
449
      provide additional examples of BSM for other different h(x). Due to space limitation, we have
      included them in Appendix B.
450
451   ▶ Finding the Breakpoint Set for h(x) ≜ λ∥x∥0
452
453
      Since the function h(x) ≜ λ∥x∥0 is scale-invariant and symmetric with ∥ ± tx∥0 = ∥x∥0 for all
      t > 0, Problem (12) reduces to the following problem:
454

                                                1+t2

               t = 0;

460   2r breakpoints { xy11 , xy22 , ..., yx2r      2r

      Squaring both sides, we obtain the following quartic equation: c4 t4 + c3 t3 + c2 t2 + c1 t + c0 = 0

470   6     E XPERIMENTS
471   This section provides numerical comparisons of OBCD against state-of-the-art methods on both
472   real-world and synthetic data. We describe the application of L0 norm-based Sparse PCA (SPCA)
473   in the sequel, while additional applications for nonnegative PCA and ℓ1 norm-based SPCA can be
474   found in Appendix J.
475   ▶ Application to L0 Norm-based SPCA. L0 norm-based Sparse PCA (SPCA) is a method that
476   uses ℓ0 norm to produce modified principal components with sparse loadings, which helps reduce
477   model complexity and increase model interpretability (d’Aspremont et al., 2008; Chen et al., 2016).
478   It can be formulated as: minX∈St(n,r) − 21 ⟨X, CX⟩ + λ∥X∥0 , where C = AT A ∈ Rn×n is the
479   covariance of the data matrix A ∈ Rm×n and λ > 0.
480   ▶ Data Sets. To generate the data matrix A, we consider 10 publicly available real-world or
481   random data sets: ‘w1a’, ‘TDT2’, ‘20News’, ‘sector’, ‘E2006’, ‘MNIST’, ‘Gisette’, ‘Caltech’, ‘Ci-
482
      far’, ‘randn’. We randomly select a subset of examples from the original data set. The size of
      A ∈ Rm×n are are chosen from the following set (m, n)∈{(2477, 300), (500, 1000), (8000, 1000),
483
      (6412, 1000), (2000, 1000), (60000, 784), (3000, 1000), (1000, 1000), (500, 1000)}.
484
485   ▶ Compared Methods. We compare with two existing operator splitting methods: Linearized
      ADMM (LADMM) (Lai & Osher, 2014; He & Yuan, 2012) and Smoothing Penalty Method (SPM)

510
      ▶ Implementations. All methods are implemented in MATLAB on an Intel 2.6 GHz CPU with
511
      32 GB RAM. However, our breakpoint searching procedure is developed in C++ and integrated into
512   the MATLAB environment 2 , as it requires inefficient element-wise loops in native MATLAB. Code
513   to reproduce the experiments can be found in the supplemental material.
514
515
      ▶ Experiment Settings. We compare objective values (F (X) − Fmin ) for different methods af-
      ter running for 30 seconds, where Fmin represents the smallest objective among all methods. For
516
      numerical stability in reporting the objectives, we use the count of elements with absolute values
517
      greater than a threshold of 10−6 instead of the original ℓ0 norm function ∥X∥0 . We set α = 10−5
518   for OBCD. Full-gradient methods have higher per-iteration complexity but require fewer iterations,
519   while OBCD, as a partial-gradient method, has lower per-iteration costs but needs more iterations.
520   Thus, we compare based on CPU time rather than iteration count.
521
      ▶ Experiment Results. Table 1 and Figure 1 display accuracy and computational efficiency results
522
      for L0 norm-based SPCA, yielding the following observations: (i) OBCD-R delivers the best per-
523
      formance. (ii) Unlike other methods where objectives fluctuate during iterations, OBCD-R mono-
524   tonically decreases the objective function while maintaining the orthogonality constraint. This is
525   because OBCD is a greedy descent method for this problem class. (iii) While other methods of-
526   ten get stuck in poor local minima, OBCD-R escapes from such minima and generally finds lower
527   objectives, aligning with our theory that our methods locate stronger stationary points.
528
529   7                   C ONCLUSIONS
530   In this paper, we introduced OBCD, a new block coordinate descent method for nonsmooth compos-
531   ite optimization under orthogonality constraints. OBCD operates on k rows of the solution matrix,
532   offering lower computational complexity per iteration for k ≥ 2. We also provide a novel optimality
533   analysis, showing how OBCD exploits problem structure to escape bad local minima and find bet-
534
      ter stationary points than methods focused on critical points. Under the Kurdyka-Lojasiewicz (KL)
      inequality, we establish strong limit-point convergence. Additionally, we present two extensions: ef-
535
      ficient subproblem solvers for k = 2 and new greedy strategies for working set selection. Extensive
536
      experiments demonstrate that OBCD outperforms existing methods.
537
538      2
           Though we prioritize accuracy over speed, the comparisons remain fair despite using different program-
539   ming languages. The other methods, based on matrix multiplication and SVD, utilize highly optimized BLAS
      and LAPACK libraries for the computational platform and compilation architecture.

      Throughout this paper, M ≜ St(n, r) denotes the Stiefel manifold, which is an embedded subman-
779
      ifold of the Euclidean space Rn×r . Boldfaced lowercase letters denote vectors and uppercase letters
780
      denote real-valued matrices. We adopt the Matlab colon notation to denote indices that describe
781   submatrices. For given natual numbers n and k, we use {B1 , B2 , ..., BCkn } to denote all the possi-
782
      ble combinations of the index vectors choosing k items from n without repetition, where Ckn is the
783   total number of such combinations and Bi ∈ Nk , ∀i ∈ [Ckn ]. For any one-dimensional function
784
      p(t) : R 7→ R, we define: p(±x ∓ y) ≜ min{p(x − y), p(−x + y)}. We use the following notations
785   in this paper.

793       • mat(x) : mat(x) ∈ Rn×r , Convert x ∈ Rnr×1 into a matrix with mat(vec(X)) = X

841   We need some prerequisite knowledge in optimization with orthogonality constraints (Absil et al.,

910   Lemma A.5. Assume QR = X ∈ Rn×n , where Q ∈ St(n, n) and R is a lower triangular matrix
911   with Ri,j = 0 for all i < j. If X ∈ St(n, n), then R is a diagonal matrix with Ri,i ∈ {−1, +1} for
912   all i ∈ [n].
913
                                 ①                                  ②
914   Proof. We derive: RRT = (QX)(QX)T = QXXT QT = I, where step ① uses R = QT X; step
915   ② uses X ∈ St(n, n) and Q ∈ St(n, n). First, given ∥R(1, :)∥ = 1 and R(1, 2 : n) = 0, we have
916   R1,1 ∈ {−1, +1}. Second, we have ∥R(2, :)∥ = 1 and R(1, :)T R(:, 2) = 0, leading to R1,2 = 0
917   and R2,2 ∈ {−1, +1}. Finally, using similar recursive strategy, we conclude that R is a diagonal
      matrix with Ri,i ∈ {−1, +1} for all i ∈ [n].


                                                          17
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


918
      Lemma A.6. We define TX M ≜ {Y ∈ Rn×r | AX (Y) = 0} and AX (Y) ≜ XT Y + YT X. For
919   any G ∈ Rn×r and X ∈ St(n, k), we have: (G − 12 XAX (G)) = arg minY∈TX M ∥Y − G∥2F .
920
921   Proof. The conclusion of this lemma can be found in (Absil et al., 2008). For completeness, we
922   present a short proof.
923
924
      Consider the convex problem: Ȳ = arg minY ∥Y − G∥2F , s.t. XT Y + YT X = 0. Introduc-
      ing a multiplier Λ ∈ Rr×r for the linear constraints leads to the following Lagrangian function:
925
      L̃(Y; Λ) = ∥Y − G∥2F + ⟨XT Y + YT X, Λ⟩. We derive the subsequent first-order optimal-
926
927
      ity condition: 2(Y − G) + X(Λ + ΛT ) = 0, and XT Y + YT X = 0. Given Λ is sym-
      metric, we have Y = G − XΛ. Incorporating this result into XT Y + YT X = 0, we obtain:
928
      XT (G − XΛ) + (G − XΛ)T X = 0. Given X ∈ St(n, r), we have XT G − Λ + GT X − ΛT = 0,
929
      leading to: Λ = 12 (XT G + GT X). Therefore, the optimal solution Ȳ can be computed as
930
      Ȳ = G − XΛ = G − 12 X(XT G + GT X).
931
932
933
      Lemma A.7. Consider the following problem: minX F ◦ (X) ≜ F (X) + IM (X), where F (X) is
934   defined in Equation (1). For any X ∈ St(n, r), it holds that dist(0, ∂F ◦ (X)) ≤ dist(0, ∂M F (X)).
935
936
      Proof. We let G ∈ ∂F (X) and define AX (G) ≜ XT G + GT X.
937
938   Recall that the following first-order optimality conditions are equivalent for all X ∈ St(n, r):
939   (0 ∈ ∂F ◦ (X)) ⇔ (0 ∈ PTX M (∂F (X))). Therefore, we derive the following results:
940               dist(0, ∂F ◦ (X))    =   inf Y∈∂F ◦ (X) ∥Y∥F = inf Y∈P(TX M) (∂F (X)) ∥Y∥F
941
                                       ①
942                                    =   ∥P(TX M) (G)∥F
943                                    ②
                                       =   ∥G − 21 XAX (G)∥F
944
                                       ③
945                                    =   ∥G − 12 X(XT G + GT X)∥F
946                                    ④
947
                                       =   ∥(I − 12 XXT )(G − XGT X)∥F
948                                    ⑤
                                       ≤   ∥G − XGT X∥F ,
949
950   where step ① uses G ∈ ∂F (X); step ② uses Lemma A.6; step ③ uses the definition of AX (G);
951   step ④ uses the identity that G − 21 X(XT G + GT X) = (I − 21 XXT )(G − XGT X); step ⑤ uses
952   the norm inequality and fact that the matrix I − 21 XXT only contains eigenvalues that are 12 or 1.

1108     1 + t ] + t λ · 1 + t2 · [⟨sign(x + ty), y⟩(1 + t2 ) − ∥x + ty∥1 t], where t◦ = (1 + t2 )−2 . We
               2      ◦

1120   ▶ Finding the Breakpoint Set for h(x) ≜ I≥0 (x)
1121
1122   Since the function h(x) ≜ I≥0 (x) is scale-invariant with h(tx) = h(x) forall t ≥ 0, Problem (12)
1123   reduces to the following problem:
1124
1125
                              t̄ ∈ arg min p(t) ≜ √a+bt
                                                    1+t2
                                                         + w+dt
                                                           1+t2 , s.t. x + ty ≥ 0.                                 (26)
                                         t
1126
1127   We define I ≜ {i|yi > 0} and J ≜ {i|yi < 0}. It is not difficult to verity that {x + ty ≥ 0} ⇔
1128   {− yxII ≤ t, t ≤ − xyJJ } ⇔ {lb ≜ max(− xyII ) ≤ t ≤ min(− xyJJ ) ≜ ub}. When lb > ub, we can
1129   directly conclude that the problem has no solution for this case. Now we assume ub ≥ lb and define
1130   P (t) ≜ min(ub, max(t, lb)). We omit the bound constraint
                                                            √               and set the gradient of p(t) to zero,
1131   which yields: 0 = ∇p(t) = [b(1 + t2 ) − (a + bt)t] · 1 + t2 · t◦ + [d(1 + t2 ) − (w + dt)(2t)] · t◦ ,
1132   where t◦ = (1 + t2 )−2 . We obtain all its real roots {t̄1 , t̄2 , ..., t̄j } with 1 ≤ j ≤ 4 after squaring
1133   both sides and solving the quartic equation. Combining with the bound constraints, we conclude that
       Problem (26) contains at most (4 + 2) breakpoints {P (t̄1 ), P (t̄2 ), ..., P (t̄j ), lb, ub} with 1 ≤ j ≤ 4.

1139   cardinality function.

                    (a) minV∈St(2,2) F (V) ≜ ∥V − A∥2F        (b) minV∈St(2,2) F (V) ≜ ∥V − B∥2F + 5∥V∥1

                                       1 0              10

1156
1157
1158   C.1   S IMPLE E XAMPLES FOR THE O PTIMALITY H IERARCHY
1159
1160
       To demonstrate the strong optimality of BS2 -points and the advantages of the proposed method, we
       examine the following simple examples of 2 × 2 optimization problems mentioned in the paper:
1161
1162                                                                        1 0
                               minV∈St(2,2) F (V) ≜ ∥V − A∥2F , with A = ( −1 −1 ).                        (27)
1163
1164
                         minV∈St(2,2) F (V) ≜ ∥V − B∥2F + 5∥V∥1 , with B = ( 11 02 ).                      (28)
1165   Figure 2 shows the geometric visualizations of Problems (27) and (28) using the relation
1166   minθ min(F (Vθrot ), F (Vθref )) = minV∈St(2,2) F (V). The two objective functions exhibit period-
1167   icity with a period of 2π. Within the interval [0, 2π), each of them contains one unique BS2 -point,
1168   while the two respective examples contain 4 and 8 critical points. Therefore, the optimality condition
1169   of BS2 -points might be much stronger than that of critical points.
1170
       BS2 -points vs. Critical Point: Algorithm Instance Study. We briefly analyze methods that find
1171   critical points of Problem (27), and demonstrate how they may lead to suboptimal results for Problem
1172   (27). We illustrate this with the notable feasible method based on the Cayley transformation (Wen
1173   & Yin, 2013). According to Equation (7) from (Wen & Yin, 2013), the update rule is defined as:
1174   Xt+1 ⇐ QXt , where Q ≜ [(I2 + τ2 A)−1 (I2 − τ2 A)]. Here, τ ∈ R, and Q ∈ R2×2 is a suitable
1175   skew-symmetric matrix. Lemma A.3 shows that the matrix Q consistently functions as a rotation
1176   matrix. Consequently, if X0 is initialized as a rotation matrix, the resulting solution Xt+1 will
1177   remain confined to this rotation matrix for all t.
1178
1179   C.2   C OMPUTING THE M ATRIX Q
1180
                                       2    2
1181   Computing the matrix Q ∈ Rk ×k as in (8) can be a challenging task because it involves the matrix
1182   H ∈ Rnr×nr . However, in practice, H often has some special structure that enables fast matrix
1183   computation. For example, H might take a diagonal matrix that is equal to LInr for some L ≥ 0
1184   or has a Kronecker structure where H = H1 ⊗ H2 for some H1 ∈ Rr×r and H2 ∈ Rn×n . The
1185
       lemmas provided below demonstrate how to compute the matrix Q.
1186   Lemma C.1. Assume (8) is used to find Q. (a) If H = H1 ⊗ H2 , we have: Q = Q1 ⊗ Q2 ,
1187   where Q1 = ZH1 ZT ∈ Rk×k and Q2 = UT     B H2 UB ∈ R
                                                            k×k
                                                                . (b) If H = LInr , we have Q =
            T
       (LZZ ) ⊗ Ik .


                                                         22
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1188
       Proof. Recall that for any matrices Ā, B̄, C̄, D̄ of suitable dimensions, we have the following equal-
1189   ity: (Ā ⊗ B̄)(C̄ ⊗ D̄) = (ĀC̄) ⊗ (B̄D̄).
1190
1191   (a) If H = H1 ⊗H2 , we derive: Q ≜ (ZT ⊗UB )T H(ZT ⊗UB ) = (ZT ⊗UB )T (H1 ⊗H2 )(ZT ⊗UB ) =
1192   (ZT ⊗ UB )T [(H1 ZT ) ⊗ (H2 UB )] = (ZH1 ZT ) ⊗ (UT
                                                         B H2 UB ) = Q1 ⊗ Q2 .
1193   (b) If H = LInr , we have: Q ≜ (ZT ⊗ UB )T H(ZT ⊗ UB ) = L(ZT ⊗ UB )T (ZT ⊗ UB ) =
1194   L(ZZT ) ⊗ Ik .

       Proof. (a) Using the results in Claim (a) of Lemma C.1, we have: (ZT ⊗ UB )T H(ZT ⊗ UB ) =

1204   L∥Z∥2sp · I.

1207   C.3   A C OMPUTATIONAL C OMPLEXITY C OMPARISON WITH F ULL G RADIENT M ETHODS
1208
1209   We present a computational complexity comparison with full gradient methods using the linear
1210   eigenvalue problem: minX F (X) ≜ 12 ⟨X, CX⟩, s.t. XT X = Ir , where C ∈ Rn×n is given.
1211
       We first examine full gradient methods such as the Riemannian gradient method (Jiang & Dai, 2015;
1212   Liu et al., 2016). The computation of the Riemannian gradient ∇M F (X) = CX − X[CX]T X
1213   requires O(n2 r) time, while the retraction step using SVD, QR, or polar decomposition demands
1214   O(nr2 ). Consequently, the overall complexity for Riemannian gradient method is N1 × O(n2 r),
1215   where N1 is the number of iterations required for convergence.
1216
       We now consider the proposed OBCD method where the matrix Q is chosen to be a diagonal matrix
1217
       as in Equality (9). (i) We adopt an incremental update strategy for computing the Euclidean gradient
1218   ∇F (X) = CX, maintaining the relationship Yt = CXt for all t. The initialization Y0 = CX0
1219   occurs only once. When Xt is updated via a k-row change, resulting in Xt+1 = Xt + UB (V −
1220   I)UT    t
           B X , we efficiently reconstruct CX
                                                 t+1
                                                      by updating Yt+1 = Yt + CUB (V − I)UT            t
                                                                                                    B X in
1221   O(nr) time. (ii) Computing the matrix P as shown in (3) involves matrix multiplication between
1222   matrices [∇f (Xt )]B: ∈ Rk×r and [[Xt ]B: ]T ∈ Rr×k , which can be done in O(rk 2 ). (iii) Solving
1223   the subproblem using small-size SVD takes O(k 3 ) time. Thus, the total complexity for OBCD is
1224   N2 × O(nr + rk 2 + k 3 ), with N2 denoting the number of OBCD iterations.
1225
1226   C.4   G ENERALIZATION TO M ULTIPLE ROW U PDATES
1227
       The proposed OBCD algorithm can be generalized to multiple row updates scheme.
1228
1229   Assume that n is an even number, and k = 2. As mentioned in Lemma 2.3, when (9) is used to find
1230   Q, the subproblem V̄t ∈ arg minV∈St(k,k) K(V; Xt , B) in Algorithm 1 reduces to:
1231
                                 min ⟨V, (∇f (Xt )[Xt ]T )BB ⟩ + h(VUB Xt ).                             (29)
1232                          V∈St(2,2)
1233
1234
       One can independently solve (n/2) subproblems, each formulated as follows:
1235   minV∈St(2,2) ⟨V, (∇f (Xt )[Xt ]T )BB ⟩ + h(VUB Xt ) with B = [1, 2].
1236
1237
       minV∈St(2,2) ⟨V, (∇f (Xt )[Xt ]T )BB ⟩ + h(VUB Xt ) with B = [3, 4].
1238   ...
1239
       minV∈St(2,2) ⟨V, (∇f (Xt )[Xt ]T )BB ⟩ + h(VUB Xt ) with B = [n − 1, n].
1240
1241   This approach, known as the Jacobi update in the literature, allows for the parallel update of n rows
       of the matrix X.


                                                         23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1242
       Notably, one can consider k ≜ |B| > 2 when h(·) = 0, and the associated subproblems can be
1243   solved using SVD.

       D     G REEDY S TRATEGIES FOR W ORKING S ET S ELECTION
1262
1263
       In this section, we introduce two novel greedy strategies designed to identify an effective working
1264
       set to enhance the practical computational efficiency of ODBC for k = 2, as shown in Algorithm
1265   2. These methods exclusively utilize the current solution Xt and its associated subgradient Gt ∈
1266   ∂F (Xt ). Notably, our subsequent discussion relies on an additional variable matrix denoted as the
1267   scoring matrix S.
1268
       Our first Working Set Selection (WSS) strategy is based on the maximum Stationarity Violation
1269
       pair, denoted as WWS-SV. It selects the index B = [ī, j̄] that most violates the first-order optimality
1270
       condition.
1271
1272   Our second working set selection strategy is rooted in the maximum Objective Reduction pair, de-
1273   noted as WWS-OR. It chooses the index B = [ī, j̄] that leads to the maximum objective reduction
1274
       under certain criteria.
1275   We have the following results for the theoretical properties of WWS-SV and WWS-OR.
1276
       Lemma D.1. (Proof in Appendix I.1, Properties of WSS-SV). Assume that the scoring matrix S
1277   is computed using (30), we have: (a) Xt ∈ St(n, r) is a critical point ⇔ S = 0. (b) S = 0
1278   ⇔ S(ī, j̄) = 0.
1279
1280
1281   Algorithm 2: WSS: Working Set Selection via Greedy Strategies.
1282   Input: Xt and Gt ∈ ∂F (Xt ).
1283   (S1) Compute the scoring matrix S ∈ Rn×n using one of the following two strategies:
1284   • Option WSS-SV (using Maximum Stationarity Violation Pair):
1285
                                          S = Xt [Gt ]T − Gt [Xt ]T .                                      (30)
1286
1287   • Option WSS-OR (using Maximum Objective Reduction Pair):
1288
                                  Sij =     min ⟨V − I2 , TBB ⟩, B = [i, j],                               (31)
1289                                      VT V=I2

1291
        where T = (Gt − Lf Xt )(Xt )T − αIn ∈ Rn×n .
       (S2) Output: B = [ī, j̄] = arg maxi∈[n],j∈[n],i̸=j |Sij |

1302   Remarks. (i) The computational complexity of both WSS-MV and WSS-OR for a given pair [i, j]
1303   is O(r). Therefore, the overall computational complexity for all C2n pairs is O(n2 r). Such com-
1304   putational complexity could be high when n is large. We consider the following more practical
1305   approach for k = 2 in our experiments. We randomly and uniformly sample p ≜ min(n, 200)
                                     C2
1306   elements from the set {Bi }i=1    n
                                            as {B̄i }pi=1 , and then we pick the working set using B = [ī, j̄] =
1307   arg maxi,j,i̸=j |Sij |, s.t. [i, j] ∈ {B̄i }pi=1 . This strategy leads to a significant reduction in computa-
1308   tional complexity to O(pr) when p ≪ C2n . (ii) When choosing k coordinates with k > 2, one can
1309   simply pick the top-k nonoverlapping coordinates according |S| as the working set.

1423
1424   Proof. Any 2 × 2 matrix takes the form V = ( ac db ). The orthogonality constraint implies that
1425   V ∈ St(2, 2) meets the following three equations: 1 = a2 + b2 , 1 = c2 + d2 , 0 = ac + bd.
1426   Without loss of generality, we let c = sin(θ) and d = cos(θ) with θ ∈ R. Then we obtain either (i)
1427   a = cos(θ), b = − sin(θ) or (ii) a = − cos(θ), b = sin(θ). Therefore, we have the following Givens
1428   rotation matrix Vθrot and Jacobi reflection matrix Vθref :
1429
1430
1431                               cos(θ) − sin(θ)          − cos(θ)          sin(θ)
                        Vθrot ≜                        ref
                                                    , Vθ ≜                             .
1432                                sin(θ) cos(θ)            sin(θ)           cos(θ)
1433
1434
1435   Note that for any a, b, c, d ∈ R, we have: det( ac db ) = ad − bc. Therefore, we obtain: det(Vθrot ) =
1436   cos2 (θ) + sin2 (θ) = 1 and det(Vθrot ) = − cos2 (θ) − sin2 (θ) = −1 for any θ ∈ R.

       Proof. Part (a). First, recall the classical Givens-QR algorithm, which is detailed in Section 5.2.5

       of (Golub & Van Loan, 2013)). This algorithm can decompose any matrix X ∈ Rn×n (not neces-
1448   sarily orthogonal) into the form X = QR, where Q is an orthogonal matrix (Q ∈ St(n, n)) and
1449
       R is a lower triangular matrix with Rij = 0 for all i < j, achieved through C2n = n(n−1)2    Givens

1453   decompose any orthogonal matrix into the form X = QR, where Q ∈ St(n, n) and R is diagonal
1454
       matrix with Ri,i ∈ {−1, +1} for all i ∈ [n].
1455   We introduce a modification to the Givens-QR algorithm, resulting in our Jacobi-Givens-QR al-
1456   gorithm as presented in Listing 1. This algorithm can decompose any matrix X ∈ St(n, n) into the
1457   form X = QR, where Q = X and R = In , using a sequence of Ckn Givens rotation or Jacobi
       reflection steps.

1474              c = 1; s = 0;                                                                                 20

1476              if (abs(b) > abs(a))                                                                          22
1477                  tau = -a/b; s = 1/sqrt(1+tauˆ2); c = s*tau;                                               23
                  else                                                                                          24
1478
                      tau = -b/a; c = 1/sqrt(1+tauˆ2); s = c*tau;                                               25
1479              end                                                                                           26
1480         end                                                                                                27
1481         V = [c s;-s c];                                                                                    28

                     Listing 1: Matlab implementation for our Jacobi-Givens-QR algorithm.
1484
1485   Please take note of the following four important points in Listing 1.
1486     a) When we remove Lines 7-10 and Lines 13-15 from Listing 1, it essentially reverts to the clas-
1487        sical Givens-QR algorithm. Givens-QR operates by selecting an appropriate Givens rotation
1488                         cos(θ) sin(θ)
            matrix V = [ − sin(θ) cos(θ) ] with a suitable rotation angle θ to zero-out the matrix element
1489        Rij systematically from left to right (j = 1 → n) and bottom to top (i = n → (j + 1)) within
1490        every pair of neighboring columns.
1491     b) Lines 7-10 and Lines 13-15 can be viewed as correction steps to ensure that the entries Rj,j =
1492        1 for all j = n.
1493     c) Line 7-10 is executed for (n − 2) times. In Line 7-10, when Jacobi-Givens-QR detects a
1494
            negative entry Ri−1,i−1 with i = j + 1, it applies a rotation matrix V ≜ ( −1    0
                                                                                          0 −1 ) to the two
1495                                             3
            rows B = [i − 1, i] to ensure that Ri−1,i−1 = 1.
1496
1497
         d) Line 13-15 is executed only once when det(X) = −1. In such cases, we have RBB = ( 10 −1      0
                                                                                                           )
            and det(RBB ) = −1, where B = [n − 1, n] is the two indices for the final rotation or reflection
1498
            step. To ensure that the resulting RBB is an identify matrix, Jacobi-Givens-QR employs a
1499
            reflection matrix V = ( 10 −1
                                        0
                                           ), leading to VT RBB = I2 .
1500

       Therefore, we establish the conclusion that any orthogonal matrix X ∈ St(n, n) can be expressed
       as D = WCkn ...W2 W1 , where Wi = UBi Vi UT                 T
                                                         Bi + UBi UBic , and Vi ∈ St(2, 2) is a suitable matrix
                                                                c
1502
1503
       associated with Bi . Furthermore, if ∀i, Vi = I2 , we have ∀i, Wi = In , leading to D = In . This
       concludes the proof of the first part of this theorem.

       Assume that the matrix X[X0 ]T has the following singular value decomposition:
1513
1514               X(X0 )T        = UDiag(z)VT , z ∈ {0, 1}n , U ∈ St(n, n), V ∈ St(n, n).
1515   Therefore, we have the following equalities:
1516
1517                                      Diag(z)    = UT X[X0 ]T V.                                 (34)
                                                               T
1518                                           D̄    = UV ∈ St(n, n).                                (35)
1519
       Furthermore, we derive the following results:
1520
1521                         z ∈ {0, 1}n
1522                   ⇒ Diag(z)T = Diag(z)Diag(z)T
1523
                       ⇒ U[Diag(z)T − Diag(z)Diag(z)T ]UT X = 0
1524
                        ①
1525                   ⇒ U[VT X0 XT U − UT X(X0 )T VVT X0 XT U]UT X = 0
1526                   ⇒ UVT X0 XT UUT X − UUT X(X0 )T VVT X0 XT UUT X = 0
1527                    ②
1528
                       ⇒ UVT X0 − X = 0
                        ③
1529                   ⇒ D̄ · X0 − X = 0,
1530
       where step ① uses (34); step ② uses UUT = In , VVT = In , XT X = Ir , and [X0 ]T X0 = Ir ; step
1531   ③ uses (35). We conclude that, for any given X ∈ St(n, r) and X0 ∈ St(n, r), we can always find
1532   a matrix D̄ ∈ St(n, n) such that D̄X0 = X.

1535   UBi Vi UT            T
                Bi + UBi UBic for some suitable Vi ∈ St(2, 2) (as established in the first part of this
                          c


1536   theorem), we can conclude that any matrix X ∈ St(n, r) can be expressed as X = D̄X0 =
1537   WCkn ...W2 W1 X0 .

       Proof. (a) The proof is similar to that of Theorem 3.6. We omit the proof for brevity.

2043   observing [ī, j̄] = arg maxi∈[n],j∈[n],i̸=j |Sij |, we can conclude that:

                                                 S(ī, j̄) = 0 ⇔ S = 0.

2137
       J     A DDITIONAL E XPERIMENTS
2138
       In this section, we present the experimental results of the proposed OBCD algorithm on the three
2139
       tasks, namely ℓ0 norm-based SPCA, ℓ1 norm-based SPCA, and Nonnegative PCA using different
2140
       working set selection strategies.
2141
2142
       J.1   A PPLICATIONS TO ℓ0 NORM - BASED SPCA, N ONNEGATIVE PCA, AND ℓ1 NORM - BASED
2143         SPCA
2144
2145   Since we have introduced ℓ0 norm-based SPCA in Section 6, we now present nonnegative PCA and
2146   ℓ1 norm-based SPCA.
2147
       ▶ Nonnegative PCA Nonnegative PCA is an extension of PCA that imposes nonnegativity con-
2148   straints on the principal vector (Zass & Shashua, 2006; Qian et al., 2021). This constraint leads
2149   to a nonnegative representation of loading vectors and it helps to capture data locality in feature
2150   selection. Nonnegative PCA can formulated as:
2151                                   min − 12 ⟨X, CX⟩, s.t. X ≥ 0,
2152                                   X∈St(n,r)
                     n×n
2153   where C ∈ R         is the covariance matrix of the data.
2154
       ▶ L1 Norm-based SPCA. As the L1 norm provides the tightest convex relaxation for the L0 -
2155
       norm over the unit ball in the sense of L∞ -norm, some researchers replace the non-convex and
2156
       discontinuous L0 norm function with a convex but non-smooth function (Chen et al., 2016; Vu
2157   et al., 2013; Lu & Zhang, 2012). This leads to the following optimization problem of L1 norm-
2158   based SPCA:
2159
                                       min − 12 ⟨X, CX⟩ + λ∥X∥1 ,
                                        X∈St(n,r)


                                                          40
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2160
       where C ∈ Rn×n is the covariance matrix of the data, and λ > 0.
2161
2162
2163
       J.2   E XPERIMENT S ETTING
2164
       We compare the objective values (F (X) − Fmin ) for different methods after running t seconds with
2165   t varying from 20 to 60, where the constant Fmin denotes the smallest objective of all the methods.
2166
2167   Initializations. We use the same initializations for all methods. (i) For the ℓ0 and ℓ1 norm-based
2168
       SPCA tasks, since the optimal solutions are expected to be sparse, we simply set X0 ∈ St(n, r)
       to an identity matrix with X0ij = 1 if i = j and otherwise 0. (ii) For the nonnegative PCA task,
2169
2170
       we use a random nonnegative orthogonal matrix as X0 , which can be generated using the following
       strategy. We first randomly and uniformly partition the index vector [1, 2, ..., n] into r nonempty
2171
       groups {Gi }ri=1 with Gi being the index vector for the i-th group, then we set X0 (Gi , i) = |G1i | for
2172
2173
       all i ∈ [r], where |Gi | is the number of elements for the i-th group.
2174   Variants of OBCD. We consider three variants of OBCD using different working set selection
2175   strategies: (i) OBCD-R that uses a simple random strategy; (ii) OBCD-CV that uses a greedy strat-
2176   egy based on maximum stationarity violation pair, and (iii) OBCD-OR that uses a greedy strategy
2177   based on objective reduction violation pair. We only consider |B| = k = 2. In order to solve the
2178   subproblem for the ℓ0 norm-based SPCA, ℓ1 norm-based SPCA, and nonnegative PCA tasks, we
2179
       use a breakpoint searching method as presented in Section 5 and Section B.
2180
2181   J.3   E XPERIMENT R ESULTS ON T HREE TASKS
2182
2183
       ▶ ℓ0 Norm-based Sparse PCA
2184   We compare OBCD against two state-of-the-art methods: (i) Linearized Alternating Direction
2185   Method of Multiplier (LADMM) (Lai & Osher, 2014) and (ii) Smoothing Penalty Method (SPM)
2186   (Lai & Osher, 2014; Chen, 2012). We also initialize OBCD-R with the result of LADMM (or
2187   SPM) and ran it for 10 seconds to evaluate its effectiveness in improving the solution, leading to
2188   LADMM+OBCD-R (or SPM+OBCD-R). To compute the subgradient Gt ∈ ∂F (Xt ) at Xt for
2189
       Algorithm 2, we choose Gt = −CXt + 0 as 0 is the subgradient of the function λ∥X∥0 .
2190   Figure 3 shows the convergence curve of the compared methods with λ = 100. Table 2, 3, and 4
2191   show the objective values (F (X) − Fmin ) for different methods with varying λ ∈ {1, 300, 1000}.
2192   Several conclusions can be drawn. (i) Due to the use of greedy strategy, OBCD-CV and OBCD-OR
2193   often lead to faster convergence than OBCD-R for this task. (ii) OBCD-R often greatly improves
2194   upon LADMM and SPM; this is because our methods find stronger stationary points than LADMM
2195
       and SPM. (iii) The proposed methods generally deliver the best performance.
2196   ▶ Nonnegative PCA
2197
       We compare OBCD against two state-of-the-art methods: (i) Linearized Alternating Direction
2198
       Method of Multiplier (LADMM) (He & Yuan, 2012; Lai & Osher, 2014) and (ii) Smoothing Penalty
2199   Method (SPM). We also initialize OBCD-R with the result of LADMM (or SPM) and ran it for 10
2200   seconds to evaluate its effectiveness in improving the solution, leading to LADMM+OBCD-R (or
2201   SPM+OBCD-R). To compute the subgradient Gt ∈ ∂F (Xt ) at Xt for Algorithm 2, we choose
2202   Gt = −CXt + 0 as 0 is the subgradient of I≥0 (Xt ).
2203
       Table 5 shows the comparisons of objective values and the violation of the constraints (F (X) −
2204
       Fmin , ∥ min(0, X)∥F + ∥XT X − Ir ∥F ) for different methods. Two conclusions can be drawn. (i)
2205   OBCD-CV and OBCD-OR are not as effective as OBCD-R in this task. This is because the matrix
2206   0 may not be a suitable choice for the subgradient for the nonsmooth function I≥0 (X). (ii) Feasi-
2207   bility of our methods is achieved with ∥ min(0, X)∥F + ∥XT X − Ir ∥F ≤ 10−12 . This is because
2208   OBCD is a feasible method. (iii) The proposed methods generally give the best performance. (iv)
2209   OBCD-R often greatly improve upon LADMM and SPM, as our methods find stronger stationary
2210   points than LADMM and SPM.
